If I then reformat drive D, then reinstall Wubi/Ubuntu on drive D, will I get my current configuration back by replacing the home.disk and root.disk created by the new install with the prior home.disk and root.disk that I have saved?   Then I presume I could use LVPM to convert to a dedicated install.

My plan is to make this a real install, with a dedicated partition, but I'd be happy to not have to go through the process of reconfiguring everything.  That is why I assume I should first go through a Wubi install, rather than installing Ubuntu directly into its own partition.  Is that correct? 

thanks for your thoughts.

Yes, in theory that should also work, but if you're reinstalling, I'd go straight to a dedicated-partition install. If you want to preserve your settings, all you need to do is copy over the contents of /home/someusername (including the hidden files/folders starting with a dot, press Ctrl-H to show) to some remote location, then after reinstalling, just paste back the contents over your new /home/someusername directory (allow it to overwrite everything), that'll keep your local user configurations (themes, app prefs, fonts, etc).

Then, if you want to avoid the hassle of reinstalling all the packages, just do this:

```
dpkg --get-selections > selections.dpkg
```

That'll generate a list of packages you have installed (back up that list to somewhere safe); after reinstalling the system, just open Synaptic, go file > read markings, select that file that was generated (selections.dpkg), press apply, and it'll reinstall all the software you have on your current system (details [here](http://www.karkomaonline.com/index.php/2006/02/24/package-management-in-debian/)).

As for the system settings (which are rather few unless you're running a server), those will simply be in /etc; like if you configured an Apache 2 webserver, it'll be in /etc/apache2, etc; do the same backup and paste back approach for those as well.

So, it'll probably be easier (and much less prone to error/corruption) if you just backup the above-mentioned local config files (/home/username/.something), system settings (/etc/something), and list of packages, then just restore all your apps, settings, and configurations after doing a clean, direct-to-partition install using [UNetbootin](http://lubi.sourceforge.net/unetbootin.html); no need to worry about quirks in Wubi or LVPM with respect to remastering Ubuntu that way (since UNetbootin-generated installs are identical to those installed using the standard CD approach).

---

### Post by nosushi4you on 2008-02-06
I finally got LVPM to work (reinstalled XP and partitioned using the disc). I have one last question. I have 3 partitions, 42 GB, 30GB, and 1GB (for swap). I picked transfer on LVPM, and now I have to choose which partition I want to install to. I want to install to my 30GB one, but I have options like  /dev/sta2, /dev/sta5, ect. How do I know which is which?

---

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-02-07
> **nosushi4you said:**
> I finally got LVPM to work (reinstalled XP and partitioned using the disc). I have one last question. I have 3 partitions, 42 GB, 30GB, and 1GB (for swap). I picked transfer on LVPM, and now I have to choose which partition I want to install to. I want to install to my 30GB one, but I have options like  /dev/sta2, /dev/sta5, ect. How do I know which is which?

It goes by number; physical partition 1 is /dev/sda1, physical partition 2 is /dev/sda2 (that numbering goes through /dev/sda4), logical partition 1 is /dev/sda5, etc.

If you don't know the partition number itself, enter the command:

```
sudo fdisk -l
```

And you should be able to determine the partition sizes through that (the "blocks" option will tell you; number of blocks will be proportional to the partition size).

Otherwise, if you need a more graphical alternative, just use GParted, it gives you the specific sizes.

PS seems like I've pinpointed down the mouse issue; it was because I was using the xvesa boot option since it seemed to have better hardware compatibility, but since the default Xorg is more robust now in PartedMagic 1.9, and xvesa's mouse driver is somewhat unreliable, I've switched back to the default Xorg in the latest build. Would be nice if someone having the mouse issue confirms that this does indeed fix the problem (as of rev120, the latest build on the sourceforge.net download page).

---

### Post by nosushi4you on 2008-02-07
So, I tried LVPM, but when I go to select Ubuntu, it says something along the lines of "Error 17: Could not mount partition." What did I do wrong?

---

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-02-07
> **nosushi4you said:**
> So, I tried LVPM, but when I go to select Ubuntu, it says something along the lines of "Error 17: Could not mount partition." What did I do wrong?

Strange, seems like GRUB didn't get configured or installed properly... Anyhow to fix it, do this:

When you get to the boot menu, press c. That'll get you into a minimalistic commandline environment. There, enter this:

```
find /boot/grub/menu.lst
```

Hopefully that should return something along the lines of (hd0,6) or so. Whatever the values for (hdx,y) are, substitute them into the following command:

```
root (hd0,6)
```

Hopefully you won't be getting an error here. If you do indeed get a Error 17 or so, that probably means the target filesystem got corrupted during the transfer for some reason, so you'll have to boot up something along the lines of Parted Magic ([download](http://sourceforge.net/project/showfiles.php?group_id=198821&package_id=240127) UNetbootin build which can be loaded from Windows) and see if you can use fsck to fix the filesystem (fsck /dev/sda2 if you specified partition 2 as the target drive, etc). However, if you don't get errors, just enter:

```
configfile /boot/grub/menu.lst
```

That'll hopefully present you with a nice menu for booting Ubuntu, select the first entry and hopefully it will work.

---

### Post by HHelsinger on 2008-02-07
Unfortunately before I got to trying your suggestion I partitioned the drive and then, using LVPM, tried to transfer my Wubi install to the new partitions.
When I try to boot into that new install I get an error message: Error 22: No such Partition.

I've attached a shot which shows how the partitions are allocated.  The Wubi install was transferred to /dev/sdb7, and that is the partition specified in GRUB.  
 ```

title		Ubuntu 7.10, kernel 2.6.22-14-generic

however, when I tried ```

```
it returned (hd0,6).

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-02-08
> **HHelsinger said:**
> Unfortunately before I got to trying your suggestion I partitioned the drive and then, using LVPM, tried to transfer my Wubi install to the new partitions.
When I try to boot into that new install I get an error message: Error 22: No such Partition.

I've attached a shot which shows how the partitions are allocated.  The Wubi install was transferred to /dev/sdb7, and that is the partition specified in GRUB.  
 ```

title		Ubuntu 7.10, kernel 2.6.22-14-generic
root		(hd1,6)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.22-14-generic root=/dev/sdb7 ro quiet splash
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.22-14-generic
```

however, when I tried ```
find /boot/grub/menu.lst
```
it returned (hd0,6).

I think the transfer was successful,  I haven't deleted my Wubi install, so I can examine the files in the new partition.  They seem to be all there.  any ideas on how I correct GRUB, if that is the problem, to recognize the partition?

Thanks for your help.

Try your luck on the super grub disk then, you can either use the physical disks at [http://linux-live-usb.org/download/Super_Grub_Disk/](http://linux-live-usb.org/download/Super_Grub_Disk/) or my loader at [http://sourceforge.net/project/showfiles.php?group_id=198821&package_id=261880](http://sourceforge.net/project/showfiles.php?group_id=198821&package_id=261880) (it's the windows .exe version you want; just install it in Windows and it'll allow you to boot it), that should fix any issues with GRUB you have (it can automatically repair GRUB, documentation at [http://linux-live-usb.org/download/Super_Grub_Disk/?section=documentation](http://linux-live-usb.org/download/Super_Grub_Disk/?section=documentation) )

EDIT: Actually, I think you can even forgo the Super Grub Disk approach, and just boot it straight out. Just do this (when in your Wubi-generated Ubuntu install):

```
sudo vol_id /dev/sdb7
```

That'll return your partition's UUID something like "2d663703-d71b-4c80-ac94-98e0dc9038c2". Write that number down.

Now, reboot, and get GRUB loaded. Press c to get into command line mode, and enter this:

```
root (hd1,6)
kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.22-14-generic root=UUID=2d663703-d71b-4c80-ac94-98e0dc9038c2 ro quiet splash
initrd /boot/initrd.img-2.6.22-14-generic
boot
```

See if that gets the job done in booting (remember to substitute the UUID you found beforehand for the one in the command). If it does get you booted in, just enter:

```
sudo grub-install /dev/sdb
```

And that'll permanently install GRUB to the MBR, hopefully solving the GRUB issues. Remember, of course, to edit /boot/grub/menu.lst and replace the Ubuntu entry towards the bottom with the command you entered in the GRUB console (after you've booted into the dedicated-partition Ubuntu install). Also remember to replace any instance of /dev/sda with /dev/sdb in that file, and any instance of (hd0,6) with (hd1,6) in case some residual errors remained in the file (to make sure the grub configfile gets properly regenerated when running update-grub).

EDIT2: Actually, after re-reading through that post, I don't think that'll get the job done either (namely because the find command is returning (hd0,6) which means GRUB is somehow thinking that the second disk is the first). Perhaps you need to change the root (hd1,6) to root (hd0,6).

Should all else fail, I think you can resort to the map command (tricking GRUB into thinking that the 2nd hard drive is the 1st), details at [http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/map.html](http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/html_node/map.html) so that this version should be able to boot it (remember to substitue the uuid in), maybe try a few variants on the root and map commands and it'll hopefully work:

```
map (hd0) (hd1)
map (hd1) (hd0)
root (hd0,6)
kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.22-14-generic root=UUID=2d663703-d71b-4c80-ac94-98e0dc9038c2 ro quiet splash
initrd /boot/initrd.img-2.6.22-14-generic
boot
```

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-02-13

I used LVPM to install into sdb7, the larger of the 2 ext3 logical partitions.   I wound up with some sort of problem in GRUB not recognizing the partition. No, there are no issues with logical partitions; I've tried using them before, and they've worked fine; instructing it to use sdb7, with your current configuration, should work fine. The standard installers likely work more reliably than LVPM does, so any issues you're having with them are probably going to occur with LVPM.

### Post by HHelsinger on 2008-02-14
I finally got around to trying your suggestions in post #210,  using your last suggestion to remap the 2nd drive to the first.  The first time is didn't work, but the second time, when I used /dev/sdb7 instead of the UUID, it worked fine.   

I'm left with a question or two:

2. I had partitioned the drive as sdb2 -- extended;  sdb5 - ext3; sdb6 - swap, and sdb7 - ext3.   Ubuntu was transferred to sdb7.  I had intended sdb5 to serve as my /home.   How do I get that to happen, and get it to mount?   Can I just rename it /home; and if so, how?

---

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-02-15
> **HHelsinger said:**
> I finally got around to trying your suggestions in post #210,  using your last suggestion to remap the 2nd drive to the first.  The first time is didn't work, but the second time, when I used /dev/sdb7 instead of the UUID, it worked fine.   

I'm left with a question or two:
1.  how do I actually save the changes to /boot/grub/menu.lst?
2. I had partitioned the drive as sdb2 -- extended;  sdb5 - ext3; sdb6 - swap, and sdb7 - ext3.   Ubuntu was transferred to sdb7.  I had intended sdb5 to serve as my /home.   How do I get that to happen, and get it to mount?   Can I just rename it /home; and if so, how? 

but anyway, many many thanks, since clearly this will work.

1. Just mount the partition from your Wubi-generated install, edit /media/disk/boot/grub/menu.lst, and it'll be the entry towards the end starting with the title "Ubuntu kernel 2.6.22-something"; add the map lines after the line beginning with "title" but before the line beginning with "kernel", then add on the special root options on the line beginning with "kernel", then save that file. Note that you're going to have to do this every time you run update-grub or update your kernel.

2. This requires you to add an entry into /etc/fstab to instruct it to mount /dev/sdb5 as /home. Afterwards, it should look something like this (substitute "someuuid" with the UUIDs for each partition the, for each you can determine the uuid via the command "vol_id -u /dev/sdb5"):

```
# /etc/fstab: static file system information.
#
# <file system> <mount point>   <type>  <options>       <dump>  <pass>
proc            /proc           proc    defaults        0       0
# /dev/sdb7
UUID=someuuid /               ext3    defaults,errors=remount-ro 0       1
# /dev/sdb5
UUID=someuuid /home           ext3    defaults        0       2
# /dev/sdb6
UUID=someuuid none            swap    sw              0       0
/dev/hdc        /media/cdrom0   udf,iso9660 user,noauto,exec 0       0

```

Before you reboot, though, move over the contents of the directory /home within /dev/sdb7 to the top-level directory of the /dev/sdb5 partition (so that the top-level directory of /dev/sdb5 should have each folder representing each user account that could be found in /home, NOT the directory /home itself). So, you should end up with an empty /home folder within the partition /dev/sdb7; that's how it should be (since the contents of /dev/sdb5 will be mounted into the /home folder), just don't delete the empty /home folder in /dev/sdb7 or otherwise /dev/sdb5 won't be able to mount due to the missing mountpoint.

---

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-04-14
> **Filigree said:**
> Except this time.  LVPM seems to be catching(halting the system and all processes) about 1/7th of the way into the transfer/copy stage.  What's the deal?

rsync (the utility that I'm using for the file copy function) is somewhat a resource hog on large loads. You should probably just leave it while it's copying the files; the system should again become usable afterwards. Or, if it's the progressbar you're referring to, it's hanging because I didn't implement file copy progress properly (would've somewhat slowed the file transfer process due to all the extra calculations and overhead of having a file copy progressbar for thousands of files and directories).

> **Filigree said:**
> I was going to attempt solution #2, however I live downtown and so use the Socialist Wireless scheme, so no landline internet connection.

Is this a common issue?  How would I go about correcting it?  

Thanks!

[UNetbootin](http://unetbootin.sourceforge.net/) can use a pre-downloaded Ubuntu ISO file (which can be downloaded from Ubuntu's website while you're still on Windows), if you have only wireless connections. If you have a (1GB or larger) USB drive, you can use the USB Drive install mode to turn your USB drive into a bootable Ubuntu LiveUSB, and then just boot and install from your USB drive. Or, if you don't have one, you can use the hard drive install mode, though you'll need to pre-partition your disk (using Parted Magic or your tool of choice) before the actual installation (after booting into Ubuntu in Live mode from your hard drive).

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-05-29

Thanks in advance !

Didier.

That's not a bug; it's intentional. I removed the "resizehome" option, since now the "resize" option copies with it the /home portion as well, so you have a single disk image that contains everything, so in other words using the resize option will give you additional space in /home. That way, issues with balancing size between the root and /home are eliminated, and disk space usage efficiency is maximized. I suppose I could again separate the resizeroot and resizehome options, but I simply don't see the point to it, since the historical advantages of an isolated /home (sharing user config and data between multiple installed distros, clean reinstallation without needing to back up user data, overcoming BIOS and OS limitations on maximum partition size, and slightly improved hard drive performance due to the location of data on the disk sectors) don't apply with a loopmounted Wubi-generated install.

---

### Post by CSEatOSU on 2008-05-29
> **tuxcantfly said:**
> That's not a bug; it's intentional. I removed the "resizehome" option, since now the "resize" option copies with it the /home portion as well, so you have a single disk image that contains everything, so in other words using the resize option will give you additional space in /home. That way, issues with balancing size between the root and /home are eliminated, and disk space usage efficiency is maximized. I suppose I could again separate the resizeroot and resizehome options, but I simply don't see the point to it, since the historical advantages of an isolated /home (sharing user config and data between multiple installed distros, clean reinstallation without needing to back up user data, overcoming BIOS and OS limitations on maximum partition size, and slightly improved hard drive performance due to the location of data on the disk sectors) don't apply with a loopmounted Wubi-generated install.

If you removed the option to only resize the entire thing at once, what would someone do if they have root and home on separate virtual disks i.e.

root.disk 30Gb
home.disk 30Gb

How would I only resize home.disk to 60Gb?  Or visa versa?

My menu.lst entries looked like this:
title		Ubuntu 8.04, kernel 2.6.24-19-generic
root		(hd0,2)/ubuntu/disks
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.24-19-generic root=UUID="421dbea3-62ab-4da4-9f29-c330d286b3a0" loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk ro splash
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.24-19-generic

There is not /ubuntu/disks directory apparently causing the boot process to stop at initramfs.

### Post by Mark Gyver on 2008-06-23
It worked, but it didn't automatically recognize my swap partition so I have some configuring left to do.  I was a bit intimidated when I saw that it said there were zero downloads on the SourceForge page for the latest release, but things seem to be fine.

I'm really impressed that it fit into such a small package and worked as smoothly as it did, but I can think of a few ways to improve it further:
[list][*]Create a command line version to reduce dependencies and make it desktop environment-neutral.
[*]Have the status show the progress on the current step so that it doesn't look like it's stalled out.
[*]Have it ask for a partition to use as swap or automatically create an appropriately-sized file to use as a virtual partition for swap like Wubi does (only on the *buntu partition instead of the Windows one).[/list]

---

### Post by tuxcantfly on 2008-06-24
> I was a bit intimidated when I saw that it said there were zero downloads on the SourceForge page for the latest release

Sourceforge's statistics servers have been down for almost a week now, that's why. If you look at the statistics pages of other sf.net projects, they will also show 0 downloads over the past week or so.

> Create a command line version to reduce dependencies and make it desktop environment-neutral.

That has already existed since the first version. When you run the lvpm script from command-line with no zenity installed, it'll default to the command-line interface, where you simply specify your target device or install mode, something like:

```
sudo lvpm /dev/sda2
```

> Have the status show the progress on the current step so that it doesn't look like it's stalled out.

That'll be a bit more tricky; zenity (what I'm using for my makeshift interface) doesn't have all the nice features full-fledged toolkits like Qt do; while I know of a couple of rather hackish approaches to implementing a standard progressbar in zenity, they slow down the file copy progress. Perhaps I'll rewrite some portions of the application, like the GUI bit, to get this implemented, though if I'm rewriting the application, there are other significant improvements I wanted to make (like combining the repartitioning and transfer portions into a single step after a single reboot, by preparing a custom initrd to load via UNetbootin), so don't expect this completed anytime soon.

> Have it ask for a partition to use as swap or automatically create an appropriately-sized file to use as a virtual partition for swap like Wubi does (only on the *buntu partition instead of the Windows one).

It actually already does search the existing swap-formatted partitions, and automatically uses it. You likely didn't have your swap properly formatted or the fdisk partition ID was not 82 (swap), that's why it didn't autodetect it. As for using a swap file, that'll introduce some of the issues Wubi suffers from (reduced performance, hibernation issues), and besides standard Ubuntu uses a dedicated swap partition by default and I want this to mimic standard Ubuntu as closely as possible, so I'd rather maintain the existing approach.

---

wubi ubuntu menu.lst:
# menu.lst - See: grub(8), info grub, update-grub(8)
#            grub-install(8), grub-floppy(8),
#            grub-md5-crypt, /usr/share/doc/grub

## default num

# the entry number 0 is the default if the command is not used.
#
# You can specify 'saved' instead of a number. In this case, the default entry
# is the entry saved with the command 'savedefault'.
# WARNING: If you are using dmraid do not use 'savedefault' or your
# array will desync and will not let you boot your system.
default		0

## timeout sec
# Set a timeout, in SEC seconds, before automatically booting the default entry
# (normally the first entry defined).
timeout 	10

# Hides the menu by default (press ESC to see the menu)

### Post by minhmeoke on 2008-10-11
@EvanRogers: Can you boot into windows, or only access the wubi-installed Ubuntu? Can you post the contents of /boot/grub/menu.lst in your Ubuntu partition?

You should have an entry for the dedicated-partition ubuntu install which looks like the following:

```
title Ubuntu 8.04.1, kernel 2.6.24-19-generic
root		(hd0,2)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.24-19-generic root=UUID=14DC5043DC502172 ro quiet splash
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.24-19-generic
```

There should also be an entry for windows:

```
title Windows
rootnoverify (hd0,0)
makeactive
chainloader +1
boot
```

I am assuming that you have windows installed on C:, a recovery partition, and Ubuntu on the third partition. If your partition configuration is different, eg windows is installed to D: or another partition, and windows does not boot, then you will have to adjust the (hd0,0) numbers to something different such as (hd0,1); first number is drive, second number is partition. You can do the same for the full-partition ubuntu install.

You can also try booting the Super Grub Disk [http://supergrub.forjamari.linex.org/](http://supergrub.forjamari.linex.org/) which may allow you to repair GRUB and be able to boot Windows and Ubuntu.

### Post by Chevron36 on 2008-11-13
Thanks for posting about LVPM worked perfectly except for a issue with GRUB.

It didn't insert which drive I'd moved ubuntu to.  This may have been me missing something or a glitch with my system but I fixed it manually by adding (hd0,2) to each ubuntu listing.  (it was previously listed as () )

EDIT:
@EvanRogers:  You may be experiencing the same error I was.  Find out which hard drive you installed to and that will be the hd0 value.  If you only have one leave it as hd0.  Look at which partition you used for root files and that will determine the ,2 portion.  Mine was the third partition (since grub is 0,1,2,3... instead of 1,2,3,4...) (you can find this information with the partition editor in wubi ubuntu intsall.)

### Post by minhmeoke on 2008-11-22
@azha,Chevron36, and others whose GRUB menu.lst contains empty () entries:
Could you post the output of
```
sudo fdisk -l
```
This is the command that LVPM parses to determine the partition setup, so if it does not show all available partitions, or are using something other than the standard /dev/hd* or /dev/sd* then the autodetection could be failing.

@Kramasha: BlipTV's security certificate apparently expired, just select "Run" and the video should appear. Alternatively you can follow the screenshot-based guide at [http://lubi.sourceforge.net/lvpm.html](http://lubi.sourceforge.net/lvpm.html) .

---

### Post by azmo35 on 2008-12-06
(SOLVED)
Hi,after having followed this 'how to',I found the error 17.To achieve that grub boot Ubuntu had to use the menu of grub "edit",and add (hd0,1) Ubuntu boot ok.
When i used Gparted live cd to create space for Ubuntu, he gave me
/dev/hda1  windows            
/dev/hda2  ubuntu          
/dev/hda3  swap
running the command $ sudo fdisk -l,returned
/dev/sda1
/dev/sda2
/dev/sda3 
Now whenever I want boot Ubuntu, I have to use the grub menu and add(hd0,1) because I think windows is (hd0,0). 	
Now my confusion,when Ubuntu boots up on the desktop i have windows partition mounted and i can't unmount, question1:is this normal? and 
question2 what i can do to mi /boot/grub/menu.1st

## altoption boot targets option
## multiple altoptions lines are allowed
## e.g. altoptions=(extra menu suffix) extra boot options
##      altoptions=(recovery) single
# altoptions=(recovery mode) single

## controls how many kernels should be put into the menu.lst
## only counts the first occurence of a kernel, not the
## alternative kernel options
## e.g. howmany=all
##      howmany=7
# howmany=all

## should update-grub create memtest86 boot option
## e.g. memtest86=true
##      memtest86=false
# memtest86=true

## should update-grub adjust the value of the default booted system
## can be true or false
# updatedefaultentry=false

## should update-grub add savedefault to the default options
## can be true or false
# savedefault=false

## ## End Default Options ##

title		Ubuntu 8.04.1, kernel 2.6.24-22-generic
root		()/ubuntu/disks
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.24-22-generic root=UUID=ad51ef36-da6e-4e45-8d6a-c989ffa69d2f  ro quiet splash

### Post by Fear_ on 2008-12-08
Just a couple of tips I wanted to share on my experience using this guide:

-If you have errors on your windows filesystem(ntfs in my case), you will need to perform a fdisk /f (in windows dos-prompt) and reboot twice before trying to partition that drive. With filesystem errors the partition manager won't let you resize the windows partition(I shrunk mine down to make room for the ex3 partition).

-After performing the transfer with lvpm, I booted to windows and ran the wubi uninstall program. I believe this changed my grub settings and I ended up having to edit grub manually to get into ubuntu. In my case, I had to set: 'root (hd0,3)' using edit in grub for ubuntu, yours may be (hd0,1) etc.

-To get the grub settings to stick, I opened a terminal window while on the gnome desktop and entered: ```
gksudo gedit /boot/grub/menu.lst
``` this brought a nice clean text editor to change my grub settings. I changed all my ubuntu boot settings for root to (hd0,3) and then took out an extra set of windows/xp boot settings at the bottom of the menu list.

Here is where I found most of my useful info: [http://grumpymole.blogspot.com/2007/05/ubuntu-how-to-edit-grub-boot-parameters.html](http://grumpymole.blogspot.com/2007/05/ubuntu-how-to-edit-grub-boot-parameters.html)

good luck!

### Post by Yashiro on 2009-01-09
Yes. The most current version of LVPM works apart from not configuring grub correctly.You will have to manually fix the new /boot/grub/menu.lst

This is probably very confusing for new folk.

Here is an example of how /boot/grub/menu.lst looks if you have the common 'Windows defector' setup (Linux using the free space on a drive Windows is currently installed upon):

```

## ## End Default Options ##

title		Ubuntu 8.04.1, kernel 2.6.24-23-generic
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.24-23-generic root=UUID=ba636202-3275-45ed-b485-da35aa659c61  ro quiet splash
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.24-23-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.04.1, kernel 2.6.24-23-generic (recovery mode)
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.24-23-generic root=UUID=ba636202-3275-45ed-b485-da35aa659c61  ro single
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.24-23-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.04.1, memtest86+
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/memtest86+.bin

title		Microsoft Windows XP Professional
root		(hd0,0)
savedefault
chainloader	+1

```

[WindowsXP(hd0,0)] - [The New Linux Partition(hd0,1)] - [Linux Swap]

If you're not very technical, or are worried something will go wrong, don't bother. The speed increase is non existent under most tasks.

### Post by Yashiro on 2009-01-12
If you need to mount your Wubi install once you've migrated you can do it quickly with this script.

Make a new text file:
```
#!/bin/sh
sudo mkdir -p /media/WindowsXP
echo "Mounting NTFS Partition"
sudo mount -t ntfs /dev/sda1 /media/WindowsXP
sleep 1
sudo mkdir -p /media/root.disk
echo "Mounting Wubi Disk"
sudo mount -o loop /media/WindowsXP/ubuntu/disks/root.disk /media/root.disk
sleep 1
echo "Done :)"
gksu nautilus /media/root.disk
exit & 0
```
Save this as **mountwubi.sh**. Right click this new file, select Permissions tab and put a tick in the Execute box.

Double click this and Run in Terminal.

### Post by IainPurdie on 2009-01-23
Unknown 4242 (nice name - though thought you were here seeking answers, not offering *THE* answer... twice ;) )

The dodgy entries in your menu.lst file can be happily deleted. If you're not sure then the easy way to test is to put a '#' before each line in each section. I had something similar.

Just make sure, if you're not positive about which one you should be booting, that you don't comment out all the options for one kernel - leave yourself the safe option or something so you can still boot up and re-edit.

If everything's fine with the commented-out version, then when you next edit just delete the section you commented if you prefer.

You may find that the sections are there for various kernel upgrades. You may find that these do exist as a result of patches and upgrades. If you want, you can ditch these via whatever package manager you use.

By running the following line in a terminal, you will be told which kernel you're actually using:

```
uname -r
```

Do a search in, say, Synaptic, for "image generic" to see which are installed. You can then uninstall/remove any *EXCEPT* the one you're booting into. A common practice is to keep the current and on old version in case their are issues with the newer one. Apparently, Synaptic will update your menu.lst and remove entries for kernels it deletes. Could be worth doing this before manually editing the file.

As ever, back the file up before messing with it.

Doing all this is quite well described on this thread:

[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1035918](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1035918)

BTW - your signature should end with the word "now", not "immediately" :)

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-01
> **ubuntubrian said:**
> In any case, I suppose I could also do the disk partitioning with a live CD and GParted? Just wondering if there's any reason not to do so?

That is correct. :) There is absolutely **no** reason not to use Gparted for partitioning. Then once you've got your hard drive partitioned, you can simply transfer your Wubi install to the new partition with LVPM, following the instructions in the first post of this thread. Couldn't be easier! ;)

Cheers! :popcorn:

-Jam man

:guitar:

EDIT: And I have used LVPM with Intrepid, and so you're not likely to have problems there. Cheers! :D

### Post by ubuntubrian on 2009-02-01

### Post by ubuntubrian on 2009-02-01
Thanks Jammanuser!!:popcorn:

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-01
> **ubuntubrian said:**
> Another thought...
I have a 150GB HDD and Gparted shows 52GB used and 88GB unused. Interestingly Disk Usage Analyzer shows the HDD as 307GB and 118GB available! What I'm trying to find out is how big my Wubi install is.

I don't have an answer to why the Disk Usage Analyzer shows a way-off size for your HDD, but as for finding out the size of your Wubi install, simply boot into Windows, and navigate to C:/ubuntu/disks where the "C" is replaced with whatever drive letter it is that your Wubi installation of Ubuntu is located. And when in that folder, select the file called "root.disk", and right-click, go to Properties, and the size of your root.disk (and hence your Wubi install, since the root.disk constitutes your whole Wubi install of Ubuntu!) will show up there.

Hope this helps! :)

-Jam man

:guitar:

### Post by Unknown 4242 on 2009-02-03
Thanks for the help and the tip on my sig IainPurdie, it helped solve one of my problems but it didn't solve the problem with my install.  I fixed it on my own though. The issue was the grub.lst file had lines like this:

root ()/ubuntu/disks

When it should read:

root (hd,0)/

### Post by IainPurdie on 2009-02-04
eskararriba:

Partition: I *think* it's because you've installed through WUBI and hence Ubuntu is not in a true partition of its own - it's in a "virtual" one.

You'll need to do as you suggested. Shrink the NTFS partition down to free space, create a brand new EXT3 partition and a SWAP in that free space.

Then use LVPM to move your Ubuntu over there.

Once you're happy with the transfer and everything works, you can, if you wish, use Windows Uninstall to remove your old WUBI version. This will free up more of your NTFS partition, allowing you to shrink it further and "donate" this space to your EXT3.

As for your files... I guess that's up to you. If you're going to store them on an external drive then I would recommend formatting that drive as FAT32 or NTFS so that non-Ubuntu machines can read it. If you're going to keep the files on your machine and never use Windowsd again then just copying them over would be fine.

Remember that Ubuntu can read files on your NTFS/FAT, but Windows won't be able to read your EXT3 partition once the files are in there.

---

### Post by IainPurdie on 2009-02-04
eskararriba: if you could find the results and post them here, that'd be great. If you're struggling with GParted there are many free Partition Managers for Windows that will do as good, if not better, a job.

[www.freewaregenius.com](www.freewaregenius.com) is a great place to check for them.

ubuntubrian: as the system says, you need to create an extended partition. I'm not on my laptop at present but if memory serves, I have:

Small system partition that Acer put there that I can't get rid of
Windows system
Windows data
Ubuntu system ext3
Ubuntu share

Last I checked, the Windows system was the primary and the rest all within the extended. A partition manager such as mentioned above should help with this - and is safer than using the Windows/DOS system to do it like in the old days!

### Post by IainPurdie on 2009-02-05
eskararriba - the utility I used did allow me to format in ext3, unfortunately I can't recall what it's called and I'm not near my laptop to check right now.

To use LVPM, as far as I'm aware, you *must* have an ext3 and a swap partition pre-available. Someone kindly correct me if this is incorrect.

However, if you use the LiveCD and create a fresh install then you should be able to tell Ubuntu where and how to install itself - possibly with the creation of partitions on empty space. In honesty, I've not gone this route (I did a WUBI install them moved with LVPM) so I can't say for certain.

You can, I think, use the LiveCD to run your limited version of Ubuntu and manually reformat the partitions you've created in Windows through it as ext3 and swap...

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-06
> **IainPurdie said:**
> eskararriba - the utility I used did allow me to format in ext3, unfortunately I can't recall what it's called and I'm not near my laptop to check right now.

To use LVPM, as far as I'm aware, you *must* have an ext3 and a swap partition pre-available. Someone kindly correct me if this is incorrect.

However, if you use the LiveCD and create a fresh install then you should be able to tell Ubuntu where and how to install itself - possibly with the creation of partitions on empty space. In honesty, I've not gone this route (I did a WUBI install them moved with LVPM) so I can't say for certain.

You can, I think, use the LiveCD to run your limited version of Ubuntu and manually reformat the partitions you've created in Windows through it as ext3 and swap...

To all who think the partition needs to be formatted as ext3 before transferring with LVPM...I can tell you that's not the case. ;) LVPM automatically formats the partition you're transferring to with the ext3 filesystem. :)

Cheers!

-Jam man

:guitar:

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-06
> **eskararriba said:**
> I forgot to mention: yes, it's a WUBI install. so I install gparted in windows, do I get that right?
(the external drive is formatted FAT, I was using it with windows.) 
thanks!

You could probably install Gparted in Windows, but the preferred method is using it from the LiveCD, while booting from it, and in the Live session. ;) Since you're not using any partitions when you use this method, that will prevent the kind of errors you might get when attempting to resize or otherwise mess with partitions that are in use by an OS. :) You can find Gparted at System>Administration>Partition editor.

Hope it helps!

-Jam man

:guitar:

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-06
> **ubuntubrian said:**
> I created a Live USB with Ubuntu disk and booted that up and started GParted. No problem shrinking the Windows partition and I can create the Swap partition. However, I run into trouble though trying to create the new Ubuntu partition as I get a message that I can have only 4 primary partitions and the Ubuntu would be the fifth. I would need extended partitions apparently. I canceled the operation and am as before.
Currently I have:
/dev/sda1   Toshiba system volume
/dev/sda2   Windows partition
/dev/sda3   HDD Recovery

So how can I proceed with this set up?

Thanks:)

This is one of the reasons why I use BING (BootIT Next Generation)...:) With BING, there is no such 4 primary partition limit...you can basically have as many as you want (the manual states that you can have even more than 200 primary partitions with BING). In case anyone's wondering how this is possible, its possible because BING uses something called an EMBR (Extended Master Boot Record) that contains its own separate partition table (called an MPT-Master Partition Table) which is what holds all the primary (as well as non-primary) partitions you have. :)

Not to mention BING has its own boot manager, imaging, (and of course partition) capabilities built right in, installed on its own separate partition, so one can use it right after turning on the computer.

BING is the tool I use to tri-boot, and I must say it is AWESOME! :D

-Jam man

:guitar:

### Post by Insane_Homer on 2009-02-13
I used LVPM to move my 32-bit 8.10 install from a wubi install (inside XP Pro Sp3) to a dedicated 120 GB HDD.

It was simple as pie, 

I installed gparted and setup the new disk with a 100GB ext3 partition and 20 GB swap then ran LVPM - which went off without a problem.

I then had a problem booting to the new partition. A Mixture of fixing the hd designation (grub menu list wanted hd1,0 when it was actually hd2,0) and then updating grub4dos to .4.4 to get around a bug with ext3.

Boot time according to bootchart has droped from 47 seconds to 38 seconds :)

chainloader +1
boot

```

And here's the contents of menu.lst on the internal drive (Which works for booting the XP install on that drive and for Wubi)

Timeout was set to 0, but I've set it to 30 to see if I can do anything there.

```
# menu.lst - See: grub(8), info grub, update-grub(8)
#            grub-install(8), grub-floppy(8),
#            grub-md5-crypt, /usr/share/doc/grub
#            and /usr/share/doc/grub-doc/.

## default num
# Set the default entry to the entry number NUM. Numbering starts from 0, and
# the entry number 0 is the default if the command is not used.
#
# You can specify 'saved' instead of a number. In this case, the default entry
# is the entry saved with the command 'savedefault'.
# WARNING: If you are using dmraid do not use 'savedefault' or your
# array will desync and will not let you boot your system.
default		0

## timeout sec
# Set a timeout, in SEC seconds, before automatically booting the default entry
# (normally the first entry defined).
timeout		30

## hiddenmenu
# Hides the menu by default (press ESC to see the menu)
hiddenmenu

# Pretty colours
color white/black green/blue

## password ['--md5'] passwd
# If used in the first section of a menu file, disable all interactive editing
# control (menu entry editor and command-line)  and entries protected by the
# command 'lock'
# e.g. password topsecret
## password --md5 $1$gLhU0/$aW78kHK1QfV3P2b2znUoe/
# password topsecret

#
# examples

# title		Windows 95/98/NT/2000

# makeactive
# chainloader	+1
#
# title		Linux
# root		(hd0,1)
# kernel	/vmlinuz root=/dev/hda2 ro

#

## lines between the AUTOMAGIC KERNELS LIST markers will be modified
## by the debian update-grub script except for the default options below

## DO NOT UNCOMMENT THEM, Just edit them to your needs

## ## Start Default Options ##
## default kernel options
## default kernel options for automagic boot options
## If you want special options for specific kernels use kopt_x_y_z
## where x.y.z is kernel version. Minor versions can be omitted.
## e.g. kopt=root=/dev/hda1 ro
##      kopt_2_6_8=root=/dev/hdc1 ro
##      kopt_2_6_8_2_686=root=/dev/hdc2 ro
# kopt=root=UUID=6CE254A8E2547872 loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio

## default grub root device
## e.g. groot=(hd0,0)
# groot=()/ubuntu/disks

## should update-grub create alternative automagic boot options
## e.g. alternative=true
##      alternative=false
# alternative=true

## should update-grub lock alternative automagic boot options
## e.g. lockalternative=true
##      lockalternative=false
# lockalternative=false

## additional options to use with the default boot option, but not with the
## alternatives
## e.g. defoptions=vga=791 resume=/dev/hda5
# defoptions=quiet splash

## should update-grub lock old automagic boot options
## e.g. lockold=false
##      lockold=true
# lockold=false

# xenhopt=

## Xen Linux kernel options to use with the default Xen boot option
# xenkopt=console=tty0

## altoption boot targets option
## multiple altoptions lines are allowed
## e.g. altoptions=(extra menu suffix) extra boot options
##      altoptions=(recovery) single
# altoptions=(recovery mode) single

## controls how many kernels should be put into the menu.lst
## only counts the first occurence of a kernel, not the
## alternative kernel options
## e.g. howmany=all
##      howmany=7
# howmany=all

## should update-grub create memtest86 boot option
## e.g. memtest86=true

# memtest86=true

## should update-grub adjust the value of the default booted system
## can be true or false
# updatedefaultentry=false

## should update-grub add savedefault to the default options
## can be true or false
# savedefault=false

## ## End Default Options ##

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-11-generic
root		()/ubuntu/disks
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=UUID=6CE254A8E2547872 loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio quiet splash 
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-11-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-11-generic (recovery mode)
root		()/ubuntu/disks

initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-11-generic

root		()/ubuntu/disks

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-7-generic (recovery mode)

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-17
> **Saghaulor said:**
> This may be more appropriate for a GRUB thread, but here goes.

Per [http://lubi.sourceforge.net/lvpm.html]("http://lubi.sourceforge.net/lvpm.html") I used the LVPM to move my Wubi install to its own dedicated partition. 

It seems to have worked correctly as said partition (sdb1) contains all of my Wubi partition files.

The problem is, when I try to boot my new Intrepid partition on (sdb1), grub reports back "Error 15: File not found". I believe grub does not know where my new Intrepid partition is. I've been digging around trying to figure out how to help grub see it, but I'm not having much luck. Some guidance would be greatly appreciated.

If its returning to you that error, then it must be because your Intrepid partition (or at least your **boot** partition, if you happen to have a separate boot partition) is not located at that address (i.e sdb1). ;) Are you **absolutely** certain that that location is correct? Remember that sdb1 (second hard drive, first partition) is not the same thing as (hd2,1), which is actually the third drive, second partition, because Grub starts the count beginning at 0, not 1, for both drives and partitions, while the sdxy notation on the other hand begins the count for partitions at 1, instead of 0.

Hope it helps.

-Jam man

:guitar:

EDIT: Oh, and Wubi installs itself **inside** the Windows partition, so obviously the Wubi location would not be the same as the new partition for Intrepid...

EDIT #2: Of course all of that is assuming that the value of **sdb1** (actually (hd2,0) if you're going by the normal syntax) has even been entered into your menu.lst located at /boot/grub/menu.lst of your Ubuntu partition...

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-17
> **jwbrase said:**
> I did try replacing ()/ubuntu/disks with (hd1,1), and still no luck.

I think there's a problem with this line:

```
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=UUID=6CE254A8E2547872 loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio quiet splash
```

but I'm not sure what I need to take out. It seems pretty obvious that I need to remove the "loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk" part of the line, but what about "root=UUID..." and "ro ROOTFLAGS=...". Do any deletions or changes need to be made there?

Replace with:

```
kernel /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=UUID=6CE254A8E2547872 ro quiet splash
```

and it should work. :)

Cheers, and I hope it helps.

-Jam man

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-19
Do you push Esc to go to the Grub menu before attempting to boot into Ubuntu?

If not, then the entry in your menu.lst that is actually being used is:

> title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-11-generic
root		(hd1,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=/dev/sdb2 ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio quiet splash 
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-11-generic

and not:

> title Ubuntu-on-/dev/sdb2
root (hd1,1)
configfile /boot/grub/menu.lst

That is because the first quote is the first entry in your menu.lst, so it is the one that is being used by default. ;) Now to clarify things here, have you tried all the entries in your menu.lst for Ubuntu to see if any of them work? 

You could try using the following in your first entry in your menu.lst instead:

> title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-11-generic
root		(hd1,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=/dev/sdb2 ro
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-11-generic

and it may work, though you need to verify that the value (hd1,1) is actually correct. You can do "sudo fdisk -l" (and that last letter is a lowercase L) to find the correct values, just as long as you remember that the sdxy notation is one number ahead for partitions, because it starts the count at 1, while Grub on the other hand begins the count at 0.

Hope it helps.

-Jam man

:guitar:

---

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-19
> **jwbrase said:**
> Of course. I've tried all entries on both drives. That's how I figured out that I could get the external drive to boot if I went to the internal one first. The only difference is that with the internal drive I press escape to get to the menu, with the external one I get to it by "pressing any key to continue" after it loads the first item by default and fails. 



I'll try that. I do know that (hd1,1) is the correct value. It's sdb2. (I formatted the swap partition first, so swap is (hd1,0), sdb1).

Yet if you boot from the external drive install **directly**, it would obviously be (hd**0**,1) for the simple reason your external drive must be first in the boot sequence in your BIOS...;)

So while you boot from the **internal** drive, sd**b**2 would of course be correct, since you're beginning the process of booting your **external** hard drive install from your **internal** hard drive, which makes it sd**b**2, instead of sd**1**2. 

So while booting your external drive **directly**, it would be (hd**0**,1) or the first hard drive, second partition, while booting from your internal hard drive, the value would be second hard drive, second partition. 

Cheers. :popcorn:

-Jam man

:guitar:

---

### Post by jwbrase on 2009-02-20
> **Jammanuser said:**
> Yet if you boot from the external drive install **directly**, it would obviously be (hd**0**,1) for the simple reason your external drive must be first in the boot sequence in your BIOS...;)

So while you boot from the **internal** drive, sd**b**2 would of course be correct, since you're beginning the process of booting your **external** hard drive install from your **internal** hard drive, which makes it sd**b**2, instead of sd**1**2. 

So while booting your external drive **directly**, it would be (hd**0**,1) or the first hard drive, second partition, while booting from your internal hard drive, the value would be second hard drive, second partition. 

Cheers. :popcorn:

-Jam man

:guitar:

Oh... #-o I thought that the drive identifiers were assigned according to some hierarchy of physical connections. The HD on the connection highest up in the hierarchy (say internal drive bay 1) gets marked as sda, the next drive gets marked as sdb, and so on. That way the drive numbers would be consistent no matter what drive you booted from.

I get it now.](*,)

```

#

#
# Put static boot stanzas before and/or after AUTOMAGIC KERNEL LIST

### BEGIN AUTOMAGIC KERNELS LIST
## lines between the AUTOMAGIC KERNELS LIST markers will be modified
## by the debian update-grub script except for the default options below

## DO NOT UNCOMMENT THEM, Just edit them to your needs

## ## Start Default Options ##
## default kernel options
## default kernel options for automagic boot options
## If you want special options for specific kernels use kopt_x_y_z
## where x.y.z is kernel version. Minor versions can be omitted.
## e.g. kopt=root=/dev/hda1 ro
##      kopt_2_6_8=root=/dev/hdc1 ro
##      kopt_2_6_8_2_686=root=/dev/hdc2 ro
# kopt=root=UUID=3286756d-b886-4541-b461-46404b2bad13 ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio

## default grub root device
## e.g. groot=(hd0,0)
# groot=(hd1,1)

## should update-grub create alternative automagic boot options
## e.g. alternative=true
##      alternative=false
# alternative=true

## should update-grub lock alternative automagic boot options
## e.g. lockalternative=true
##      lockalternative=false
# lockalternative=false

## additional options to use with the default boot option, but not with the
## alternatives
## e.g. defoptions=vga=791 resume=/dev/hda5
# defoptions=quiet splash

## should update-grub lock old automagic boot options
## e.g. lockold=false
##      lockold=true
# lockold=false

## Xen hypervisor options to use with the default Xen boot option
# xenhopt=

## Xen Linux kernel options to use with the default Xen boot option
# xenkopt=console=tty0

## altoption boot targets option
## multiple altoptions lines are allowed
## e.g. altoptions=(extra menu suffix) extra boot options
##      altoptions=(recovery) single
# altoptions=(recovery mode) single

## controls how many kernels should be put into the menu.lst
## only counts the first occurence of a kernel, not the
## alternative kernel options
## e.g. howmany=all
##      howmany=7
# howmany=all

## should update-grub create memtest86 boot option
## e.g. memtest86=true
##      memtest86=false
# memtest86=true

## should update-grub adjust the value of the default booted system
## can be true or false
# updatedefaultentry=false

## should update-grub add savedefault to the default options
## can be true or false
# savedefault=false

## ## End Default Options ##

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-11-generic
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=UUID=3286756d-b886-4541-b461-46404b2bad13 ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio quiet splash 
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-11-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-11-generic (recovery mode)
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-11-generic root=UUID=3286756d-b886-4541-b461-46404b2bad13 ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio  single
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-11-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-7-generic
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-7-generic root=UUID=3286756d-b886-4541-b461-46404b2bad13 ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio quiet splash 
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-7-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.10, kernel 2.6.27-7-generic (recovery mode)
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.27-7-generic root=UUID=3286756d-b886-4541-b461-46404b2bad13 ro ROOTFLAGS=syncio  single
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.27-7-generic

title		Ubuntu 8.10, memtest86+
root		(hd0,1)
kernel		/boot/memtest86+.bin

### END DEBIAN AUTOMAGIC KERNELS LIST

# This is a divider, added to separate the menu items below from the Debian
# ones.
title		Other operating systems:
root


# This entry automatically added by the Debian installer for a non-linux OS
# on /dev/sda2
title		Microsoft Windows XP Professional
root		(hd1,1)
savedefault
chainloader	+1



# This is a divider, added to separate the menu items below from the Debian
# ones.
title		Other operating systems:
root


title Windows
root (hd1,1)
makeactive
chainloader +1
boot
```

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-02-20
> **jwbrase said:**
> Oh... #-o I thought that the drive identifiers were assigned according to some hierarchy of physical connections. The HD on the connection highest up in the hierarchy (say internal drive bay 1) gets marked as sda, the next drive gets marked as sdb, and so on. That way the drive numbers would be consistent no matter what drive you booted from.

I get it now.](*,)

No, it locates the drives you enter into your menu.lst from the BIOS, which is higher up the food chain so to speak, meaning that the physical connection has little bearing on the booting process, since the BIOS is capable of moving drives around in the boot sequence regardless of which drive is plugged into which port. How exactly that takes place though, I'm not sure, and is something I have wondered about for a long time. 

Cheers. :popcorn:

-Jam man

:guitar:

EDIT: Glad to see its working now (except for Windows, that is...). :D

### Post by jwbrase on 2009-02-20
Tada!!!!

I've finally got everything set up as I want it.

What I was missing was that Windows won't boot on anything but the first disk. So the format of the correct entry is as follows:

```
title		Microsoft Windows XP Professional
map (hd0) (hd1)
map (hd1) (hd0)
root		(hd1,1)
chainloader	+1
```

Amazing what reading the documentation can do:

[http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/grub.html#DOS_002fWindows](http://www.gnu.org/software/grub/manual/grub.html#DOS_002fWindows)


So I'm now completely ported over to an Ubuntu install on a physical drive and have uninstalled Wubi.

---

### Post by ashour on 2009-03-24
does this means that it will wipe out windows?
can't it move it to a real partition and dual boot it with windows?

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-03-24
> **ashour said:**
> does this means that it will wipe out windows?
can't it move it to a real partition and dual boot it with windows?

It will only wipe out Windows if you accidentally tell it that the partition to transfer to is your host OS partition, i.e. the partition that contains your Windows 0S, and the Wubi virtual OS. ;)

For that reason, the tool specifically tells you which partition contains your host OS (and Wubi), so you don't select it by mistake. :)
FYI, when LVPM transfers your Wubi install over to a dedicated partition, it writes Grub to the MBR at the same time, which means if your Windows bootloader is currently controlling the boot (using EasyBCD, for example), it will replace the Windows boot code in the MBR with Grub, though that's not really a problem since Grub can boot Windows just fine. It just depends on which OS's bootloader you want controlling the boot, the Vista one (assuming you're using Vista, and not XP), or Grub.

Cheers.

-Jam man

:guitar:

---

### Post by blade1950 on 2009-04-05
I found this on the net. I hope it helps. [http://www.linux.com/feature/121916](http://www.linux.com/feature/121916). I've been playing with WUBI for about a month and plan to dump MS COMPLETELY by the end of next month. As for MS needing the first disk, it's actually the first track that it needs in order to boot. It can boot from almost any HDD but ONLY from track 0 (zero) of that HDD. So if ANYTHING is on that track, MS will NOT boot. That is, of course if the BIOS is not an antique. As far as I know MS is the only OS with that "HARD WIRED" requirement and it is from sloppy, lazy programmers. Remember, when MS first started out they were writing code for IBM almost exclusively and IBM wanted **[SIZE="7"]BIG FAT[/SIZE]** applications that took up LOTS of space so they could sell more equipment to run it. That's why you can find all kinds of "Easter eggs" in MS code. Don't get me wrong BG is a smart businessman but as SJ says, not very creative or innovative.

---

### Post by hintapalinta on 2009-04-07
Just in case this is helpful for others who are trying to move the wubi installation of (k)ubuntu to a dedicated partition and read this thread (as I did yesterday night): It worked on my Thinkpad x60 (originally XP, now dual booting) after the following little tinkering.

LVPM installed Kubuntu from wubi to /dev/sda3, but somehow the bootloader didn't know this and looked for Kubuntu on the first partition (which is Windows XP, ntfs), so I got Error Message 17 (cannot mount the partition).  I logged into Kubuntu (still had the wubi installation) and went to System Settings / Advanced / GRUB editor and edited the first entry (Ubuntu 8.10); changed Root to (hd0,2).  I read somewhere that this may resolve the issue, and it did.

I'm a happy (k)ubuntu newbie now.  Aside: I switched because a windows update (or something else, I don't really know) was causing problems with networking, and I am extremely happy with how easy the transition to ubuntu is made by wubi and LVPM, and how well (K)ubuntu works.  Thanks to all who worked on / contributed to these projects.

### Post by adromidon on 2009-06-13
While I know it is not officialy supported yet for 9.04 is it possible to use this on 9.04 i mean will it function or is it even worth trying to use LVPM with 9.04

---

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-06-14
> **ubuntubrian said:**
> Ah, so the link gets truncated automagically.....
Anyway, follow the shorter link and it's right there.

that guide also says to not install to root partition unless you want to get rid of windows entirely. I don't want/need windoze so do I still need a new swap partition if I transfer to the root partition?
If you actually mean the "host" (not "root") partition, i.e. the Windows partition Wubi installed Ubuntu to, then I think you're not supposed to transfer to that partition because it will likely cause trouble since Ubuntu itself (what you're transferring!) is installed there. I believe LVPM formats the partition it transfers to with ext3 filesystem before it transfers, so you will trash your Ubuntu (not just Windows) if you do that, and I imagine you don't want to do that...;)
Transfer to a different partition (resizing the host partition if you have to, to free up some space for the new partition), and yes, you will need a swap partition (at least if you're planning to hibernate).

---

When I mount and view the 9GB partition and the transferred files I suspect that the "default" file inside the /boot/grub directory may be corrupt as it's type is "TGA image (image/x-tga)". Would this cause the problem? Also I only have **read** permission on this partition from the Wubi install.

Any help would be greatly appreciated.

### Post by IainPurdie on 2009-08-13
You'll only have "read" because WUBI will be installed (or thinks it's installed) on that partition. This is the usual behaviour, so you'll likely have to edit the grub.lst file from DOS or Windows.

I'm guessing that the aforementioned file is either corrupt or just not configured properly. I've had this before. Any chance you could copy/paste the contents of this file and pop it up here?

---

### Post by wackzingo on 2009-08-13
I copied the **default** from the Wubi install to the one on the real partition using **sudo** and it worked. It simply has a **0** at the beginning and a warning in comments below.

Here is the menu.lst from the install on the real partition not the Wubi one.
```

# menu.lst - See: grub(8), info grub, update-grub(8)
#            grub-install(8), grub-floppy(8),
#            grub-md5-crypt, /usr/share/doc/grub
#            and /usr/share/doc/grub-doc/.

## default num
# Set the default entry to the entry number NUM. Numbering starts from 0, and
# the entry number 0 is the default if the command is not used.
#
# You can specify 'saved' instead of a number. In this case, the default entry
# is the entry saved with the command 'savedefault'.
# WARNING: If you are using dmraid do not use 'savedefault' or your
# array will desync and will not let you boot your system.
default		0

## timeout sec
# Set a timeout, in SEC seconds, before automatically booting the default entry
# (normally the first entry defined).
timeout		3

## hiddenmenu
# Hides the menu by default (press ESC to see the menu)
hiddenmenu

# Pretty colours
#color cyan/blue white/blue

## password ['--md5'] passwd
# If used in the first section of a menu file, disable all interactive editing
# control (menu entry editor and command-line)  and entries protected by the

### Post by wackzingo on 2009-08-14
I used this successfully on the latest version of Wubi version and Ubuntu 9.04.

I figured out the solution from reading this thread. Everything went fine until rebooting when I was getting **Error Code 17** and said it couldn't mount the drive. I had to boot back into the Wubi install, open terminal and execute this command:

**sudo gedit /media/disk/boot/grub/menu.lst**

The path may need to be changed to the menu.lst on the permanent install. Once it's open in the editor I found the following code:(this is only part of it.) 

```

## ## End Default Options ##

title		Ubuntu 9.04, kernel 2.6.28-15-generic
root		()/ubuntu/disks
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.28-15-generic root=UUID=38ff48de-bd84-4e2a-a99d-b48cb59b6612  ro quiet splash 
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.28-15-generic

```

I had to change the

```
root		()/ubuntu/disks
```

Change **()/ubuntu/disks** to **(hd0,2)**. This will be different for each person. I had three partitions:

```

/dev/sda1/ is my Wubi install.
/dev/sda2/ is the swap partition.
/dev/sda3/ is the real partition for Ubuntu.

```

(Please correct me if I'm wrong on the information below)
I believe (hd0) refers to the first hard drive since counting starts at '0'. I then wanted it to boot from **/dev/sda3** because that is the permanent installation. Again because counting starts at '0' **sda1 is 0, sda2 is 1 and sda3 is 2** which is why I chose (hd0,2)

Hope this helps.

---

### Post by hhh on 2009-08-19
Wubi-edit-/LVPM works with 9.04, but it's busted, it doesn't write /boot/grub/menu.lst correctly, as far as I can tell. "Reboot to use your new Ubuntu install!" or whatever and you get... Error 17! Hit any key to be totally frustrated! YAY! HOW SATISFYING! NEXT STEP FOR US NOOBIES (and I did it 2 years ago) IS REFORMAT YOUR HARD DRIVE! OH JOY!

When you hit any key, you get the boot menu, and all your Ubuntu entries, all eight of them or whatever, all don't work, right? Go to the top one, hit Enter, now you can edit that entry. It should say...
```
# root		()/ubuntu/disks
```
Type the letter e to edit that line, backspace to erase the ()ubuntu/disks part of it, enter your install partition, for example you have Windows installed, you created a swap in the middle, Ubuntu partition at the end. So, Windows is (hd0,0), swap is (hd0,1) so you want to enter (hd0,2), so the line reads root (hd0,2) for example. Hit Enter, then b to boot. You're in. Bra... vo. Fix your menu.lst so that doesn't happen again, so...
Applications>Accessories>Terminal, type gksu nautilus because Linux/Ubuntu is so freaking retarded about privelages, now you can completely ruin your Ubuntu install if you delete the wrong thing, SO DON'T. Just navigate to /boot/grub and open menu.list, find the top entry that says ```
# root		()/ubuntu/disks
```, change it to what you did before, save the file. If you want, add # before kernel entries you don't want to see on boot, then they'll still be there if you need to uncomment them, that's better than deleting them and not remembering what they were. Now just have fun getting your video driver going! (Hint, the documentation says /etc/X11/xorg, but it's wrong, that's been changed to xorg.conf, all the docs are 3 years out of date it seems, yay new release every six months! Keeps everyone on their toes!) You're almost there, now just disable/blacklist pcspkr to shut off that annoying triple beep at shutdown that has your co-workers on Windows laughing at you! Again, the gurus say you can find the file at /etc/modprobe.d/blacklist, but they lie, those jerks! It's also been changed to blacklist.conf! Who cares if that beep might tell you if your fan is busted and your motherboard is melting, it puts hair on your chest! And reduce the speed on your wireless usb adapter to 11M via sudo iwconfig wlan0 rate 11M, because it's 4 times faster that way, even though Network Manager says it's running at 54M! wOOt! And forget about getting the Gnome Main Menu applet to open before a 3 second delay after log-in, it's not going to happen! Joy! I'd much rather have "Applications Places System" staring me in the face all the time, because that applet works and the other doesn't who cares! It's a low priority bug! A freaking 5 year old or something bug, hurrah! Start downloading packages, time to learn about dependencies! HAPPY HAPPY! Make your choice, Add/Remove Programs! Synaptic! Apt-get! Aptitude! .DEB! RPM! TAR.BZ2! DPKG! AAAAAAAAAAHHHHHHHHH!!!!!!!


Just another Ubuntu fanboy post, I'll never go back, I swear. I made it this far, didn't I?

Mozilla/5.0 (X11; U; Linux i686; en-US; rv:1.9.2a2pre) Gecko/20090818 Namoroka/3.6a2pre

### Post by hhh on 2009-08-19
So, just to follow up before I get banned, and I am, in my opinion, not really knowledgeable to give advice, but some of you are FLOUNDERING, so what the hell...

Read this whole post before trying anything, the info came out semi-stream of consciousness...

BEFORE YOU OR ANYONE ELSE, ALL OF YOU, PAY ATTENTION, DOES ANYTHING, and this little tip has saved my keester many times, CREATE A SUPER GRUB DISC...
[http://www.supergrubdisk.org/](http://www.supergrubdisk.org/) ... SO YOU CAN EASILY RESTORE THE WINDOWS BOOT MENU IF YOU SCREW UP (ie, Ubuntu didn't install right but Grub did and now you can't boot into Windows. The *Fix Boot of Windows* tool on that disc is INVALUABLE, meaning FANTASTIC, YOU WANT TO HAVE IT, IT'S A GOOD THING!

TO MAKE YOUR PARTITIONS, USE [http://unetbootin.sourceforge.net/](http://unetbootin.sourceforge.net/) A decent rule for swap is equal to your RAM, but 2G max if you have more than 2G RAM. There's apparently a lot of argument about this, whatever, it's the rule I've seen most. If I open System Monitor I almost never see swap being used, and I have 1G swap and a lowly 1G RAM, and my system is running beautifully.

Before you repartition Windows, DE-FRAGMENT!!!! AFTER YOU REPARTITION WINDOWS, MEANING YOU'VE JUST SHRUNK YOUR WINDOWS PARTITION, THE VERY FIRST THING YOU SHOULD DO IS REBOOT BACK INTO WINDOWS AND IT SHOULD AUTOMATICALLY RUN CHECK DISC, PLEASE LET IT DO SO. Everything I've ever seen about repartitioning says this. Then you can do your LVPM kung fu with less worries of Windows going bye bye.

@polkadotteapot, just moving the kernel up the list isn't changing anything, you have to edit the ```
root ()/ubuntu/disks
``` line of the top kernel listed in your boot menu to get into your actual install. Right now, if I understand you, you are still in your Wubi install (Wubi=Windows Ubuntu Install), not your real Ubuntu install. So, after rebooting, hit Esc when your computer starts to load Grub, then edit the top kernel listed as I described in my post above. Now you are booting into your real Ubuntu install, gksu nautilus to open files for editing (AGAIN, CAUTION!!! NOW YOU HAVE ROOT PRIVELAGES MEANING YOU CAN DESTROY STUFF AT WILL), edit /boot/grub/menu.lst as I described in my post above changing the root line directly below ```
## ## End Default Options ##
``` in that file and save it so you don't have to edit the boot menu every time you restart your computer.

@Ron, a 5GIG swap? What the hell for, do you have like 2 kbs of RAM or something? 2G is the max I have seen recommended for modern computers. Whatever, leave it alone, it's fine and won't hurt anything, but you posted that the partition you made for Ubuntu is on sda3, but you seem (to me, I could easily be wrong, like I said) to be transferring to sda2, your swap partition *see what you posted? ...To transfer an install to a dedicated partition **(/dev/sda2)**... Shouldn't that be sda3, as you also posted?*. Redo your partitions and LVPM Wubi to the right partition. 

@Tootler, congrats, too bad the link you posted is already dead, I bet that's frustrating the hell out of some people, like candtalan. Ah, I see that it's fixed in ubuntubrian's post, but THAT LINKED POST IS PRETTY SPARCE, ISN'T IT? I hope I'm filling in some of the blanks here.

@ubuntubrian, for Pete's sake, keep Windows till you know everything's working OK! And then keep it longer! Hard drive space is cheap these days! I boot to Windows rarely, but it's great to have the option.

@adromidin, it will work, you just have to fix Grub. Also, get your video card issues and update Ubuntu WHILE YOU ARE STILL IN WUBI, that saves a lot of headaches in my experience. Plus, and I haven't ever tried to triple boot and I have no experience with Fedora, but it looks like you have the partition numbers backwards. hd0 means the first hard drive on your computer, (hd0,0) means first drive, first partition, (hd0,1) means second partition, etc... My computer only has one drive, so someone correct me if I'm wrong.

BTW, my cheapo computer had a recovery partition listed as drive D: in Windows, but it was actually just a partition at the end of the only drive on my computer, and I wiped it by accident. Try to avoid the same mistake if your computer is set up the same way.

@SPARTAN, it will work, just fix /boot/grub/menu.lst

@wackzingo, you have it, but for anyone not getting it yet...
```
# ## End Default Options ##

title		Ubuntu 9.04, kernel 2.6.28-15-generic
root		()/ubuntu/disks
kernel		/boot/vmlinuz-2.6.28-15-generic root=UUID=38ff48de-bd84-4e2a-a99d-b48cb59b6612  ro quiet splash 
initrd		/boot/initrd.img-2.6.28-15-generic
```
Everyone's starting to get the hang of this, right? root ()/ubuntu/disks needs to be root (hd0,2) as that's how he has described his partitioning... (hd0,2)=/dev/sda3. Presto, working Linux. Change the other Linux root entries to the same thing (well, the ones you want, I kept the most recent kernel, plus the most recent kernel recovery, and commented out the rest with # . If I ever want to do a mem test I can just uncomment that entry and reboot).

@abdusamed, what? Virtual driver, what? I'll just tackle the part I think I understand... LVPM just transfers Wubi to a dedicated partition, it does not create or re-size partitions. For that, use [http://unetbootin.sourceforge.net/](http://unetbootin.sourceforge.net/) . Install it on Windows after you create your Super Grub Disc (not required, but highly recommended!), then the next time you reboot it will be an option in your Windows boot menu. If you reboot to Windows again, the first thing you'll see when your dekstop comes up is "Are you sure you want to remove unetBootin and all of it's components?" or something, I always choose no so that option is still in the Windows portion of the GRUB boot menu. You can always uninstall it later by running the unetbootin file from Windows again

I hope I haven't spread any misinformation here, there is enough of that floating around the Ubuntu community already, so please post if you catch a mistake on my part. I hope that helps, and I'm ready for my banning now.

Cheers.

### Post by Jammanuser on 2009-12-01
LVPM writes Grub to the MBR, and *should* have automatically added Ubuntu to the first boot menu. The reason you don't have the Ubuntu entry in the Windows boot menu anymore is because it was deleted when you unistalled Wubi (and besides, that entry would have still booted the Wubi install, not the one you transferred with LVPM to a dedicated partition, if you hadn't uninstalled Wubi).

What happens when you select the "Unknown OS" entry in the first boot menu?

Acer provisioned the unit with 3 partitions.  Recovery (17G or so), Boot (100M or so) for windows 7 startup and the windows "C:" partition for the remainder of the drive (abt. 160G total capacity).

I have decided not to abandon Ubuntu (yet) and so, want a more reliable, more standard, probably faster install.  I have tried all day to make LVPM move my install but it fails every time!

I am using the instructions at the beginning of this thread.  I have also searched for better solution and tweaks to the process.  I had already resized(shrunk) my sda3 (the C drive) to allow space to set up swap and os partiton.  I formatted that space (tried two ways -no swap - all ext3 as the last fo the four allowed (primary?) partitions, and as extended with 8G swap (smallest allowed) and 30G as ext3) behaivior of LVPM was same in both cases. : Sudo, select transfer, the operation would start, I would select the sdax that I had verified prior was the correct sda and the entire process would either be done in 30 seconds or many minutes (system monitor would show heavy cpu activity in either case during the "format and move" with a reboot msg at the end but on reboot, no changes have been made to boot process and the volume with the transferred info in it is corrupt, unreadable etc.

EditOff--

### Post by kadu01 on 2010-01-19
I tried convert to 9.1 from loopback on my primary HDD to secondary one with full ext3. I found issue in the lvpm script.

It uses vol_id to get some disk information and modify files like /etc/fstab, grub (menu.lst, device.map) and /etc/initramfs-tools/config.d.resume ... so it won't get correct values and due to this will not work correctly. There is need to "remake it" to use blkid instead of vol_id. 

I hope it will be helpfull for creating new update.

The happy message is that I successfully migraded from loopback under my windows to standalone partition ;--) So it works under 9.10 with manual interecption

Regards

kadu

---

### Post by pwhalley on 2010-01-19
OK.. 

Sorry for the delay in posting my "fix" for my own stupidity.  You can probably guess where I am going here.

My primary problem with conversion was inattention to sizing of the new partition size - G vs M.  30 Mega Bytes VS 30 Giga Bytes.  

There are still issues with dual boot and the way 9.10 and grub2 handles booting with Win7.  Every kernel update breaks the boot process.

Peter

### Post by Rimus on 2010-06-05
I tried this yesterday with a 10.04 Wubi install and I ran into problems with grub as well. Grub actually* was* installed to MBR, but there was no Ubuntu option, as if the menu list was never updated (there was only -- other OS's -- and Vista). Based on this experience **I recommend not to use LVPM with 10.04 unless you're familiar with grub rewriting.** I ended up installing 10.04 from the cd since I ran out of patience.

Here's info about rewriting grub 2 (the version 10.04 has):
[URL="https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Grub2#Reinstalling%20from%20LiveCD"]https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Grub2#Reinstalling%20from%20LiveCD
[/URL] 
Be sure to check the post-restoration commands as well if you just can't get it to work:
[https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Grub2#Post-Restoration%20Commands](https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Grub2#Post-Restoration%20Commands)

If I was to do this all over again, I'd probably do a clean Ubuntu 10.04 install, then use[ this script]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1037874") to mount the wubi filesystem and copy all the files in wubi /home to the actual partition. I'd still lose the installed packages though.

### Post by yolucid on 2010-06-06
here's  solution from my experience ... just manage to complete transfer lucid into real partition today.:P

> **frank1e said:**
> Guys I need some help with LVPM if any of yall are real LVPM knowledgable or linux knowledgeable please help. I'm not doing a cd install because it freezes every time. Some kind of bug a lot of people are having and none of the fixes have worked.  Ok hardware info is Win XP on C drive, D drive is formatted with ext 3 partition. This is where im trying to move it to.  

If you can boot ubuntu live cd until it finish loading and you can see the complete ubuntu linux, there are great chances that you will be able to see start menu > system > administration > startup disk creator. Provided you have a decent 2gig or more usb thumb drive, you can start cd less installation from there 

    > **frank1e said:**
>  Now I did LVPM Transfer.  Everything seemed to go well but when I restarted I got a screen that said press S to skip mounting or M for manual recovery.  I hit S and Ubuntu booted up.  Now I do not know how to tell whether I am booted off my old WUBI install or if I am now booted off the D drive.  I know that it didnt mount the D drive and when I try to mount it it says I have to be root to do it.  
   So first off can yall tell me how to check whether Im on my wubi install or if Im working off D drive and just need to mount it?  And second if Im still on my wubi install how do I make lvpm work.  Any help much appreciated.

It sounds that you already solve most of your transfer process. You can check at start menu system > administration > system monitor and it tells about your system more.

As for the S or M, you may have to edit the /etc/fstab file as to point out to the right device mounting process.
